    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41438"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The genome of Chinese flowering cherry ( Cerasus serrulata ) provides new insights into Cerasus species"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Horticulture Research 2020 7:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-10-01"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Cerasus serrulata is a flowering cherry germplasm resource for ornamental purposes. In this work, we present a de novo chromosome-scale genome assembly of C. serrulata by the use of Nanopore and Hi-C sequencing technologies. The assembled C. serrulata genome is 265.40&#8201;Mb across 304 contigs and 67 scaffolds, with a contig N50 of 1.56&#8201;Mb and a scaffold N50 of 31.12&#8201;Mb. It contains 29,094 coding genes, 27,611 (94.90%) of which are annotated in at least one functional database. Synteny analysis indicated that C. serrulata and C. avium have 333 syntenic blocks composed of 14,072 genes. Blocks on chromosome 01 of C. serrulata are distributed on all chromosomes of C. avium, implying that chromosome 01 is the most ancient or active of the chromosomes. The comparative genomic analysis confirmed that C. serrulata has 740 expanded gene families, 1031 contracted gene families, and 228 rapidly evolving gene families. By the use of 656 single-copy orthologs, a phylogenetic tree composed of 10 species was constructed. The present C. serrulata species diverged from Prunus yedoensis ~17.34 million years ago (Mya), while the divergence of C. serrulata and C. avium was estimated to have occurred &#8764;21.44 Mya. In addition, a total of 148 MADS-box family gene members were identified in C. serrulata, accompanying the loss of the AGL32 subfamily and the expansion of the SVP subfamily. The MYB and WRKY gene families comprising 372 and 66 genes could be divided into seven and eight subfamilies in C. serrulata, respectively, based on clustering analysis. Nine hundred forty-one plant disease-resistance genes (R-genes) were detected by searching C. serrulata within the PRGdb. This research provides high-quality genomic information about C. serrulata as well as insights into the evolutionary history of Cerasus species."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p><i>Cerasus serrulata</i> is a flowering cherry germplasm resource for ornamental purposes. In this work, we present a de novo chromosome-scale genome assembly of <i>C. serrulata</i> by the use of Nanopore and Hi-C sequencing technologies. The assembled <i>C. serrulata</i> genome is 265.40âMb across 304 contigs and 67 scaffolds, with a contig N50 of 1.56âMb and a scaffold N50 of 31.12âMb. It contains 29,094 coding genes, 27,611 (94.90%) of which are annotated in at least one functional database. Synteny analysis indicated that <i>C. serrulata</i> and <i>C. avium</i> have 333 syntenic blocks composed of 14,072 genes. Blocks on chromosome 01 of <i>C. serrulata</i> are distributed on all chromosomes of <i>C. avium</i>, implying that chromosome 01 is the most ancient or active of the chromosomes. The comparative genomic analysis confirmed that <i>C. serrulata</i> has 740 expanded gene families, 1031 contracted gene families, and 228 rapidly evolving gene families. By the use of 656 single-copy orthologs, a phylogenetic tree composed of 10 species was constructed. The present <i>C. serrulata</i> species diverged from <i>Prunus yedoensis</i> ~17.34 million years ago (Mya), while the divergence of <i>C. serrulata</i> and <i>C. avium</i> was estimated to have occurred <span class="stix">â¼</span>21.44 Mya. In addition, a total of 148 MADS-box family gene members were identified in <i>C. serrulata</i>, accompanying the loss of the AGL32 subfamily and the expansion of the SVP subfamily. The MYB and WRKY gene families comprising 372 and 66 genes could be divided into seven and eight subfamilies in <i>C. serrulata</i>, respectively, based on clustering analysis. Nine hundred forty-one plant disease-resistance genes (R-genes) were detected by searching <i>C. serrulata</i> within the PRGdb. This research provides high-quality genomic information about <i>C. serrulata</i> as well as insights into the evolutionary history of <i>Cerasus</i> species.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p><i>Cerasus serrulata</i> (Lindley) Loudon belongs to <i>Cerasus</i> Mill. in the Rosaceae family<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Du, H. K. Practical methods for rapid seed germination from seed coat-imposed dormancy of Prunus yedoensis. Scientia Hortic. 243, 451â456 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d50938e660">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Balsamo, R. A. et al. Leaf biomechanics, morphology, and anatomy of the deciduous mesophyte Prunus serrulata (Rosaceae) and the evergreen sclerophyllous shrub Heteromelesarbutifolia (Rosaceae). Am. J. Bot. 90, 72â77 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d50938e663">2</a></sup>. The corymbose-racemose or subumbellate inflorescences of this species usually have 2â3 (5) flowers, and most flowers are white or pale pink, with single petals<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Liu, Z. X. et al. Development of stamens and carpels in single and double flowers of Cerasus serrulata. J. Beijing For. Univ. 32, 486â491 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d50938e667">3</a></sup>. Trees of <i>C. serrulata</i> (<i>Cerasus serrulata</i>) have dark green leaves with acuminate serrate teeth on the margins, dense flowers with unfolding petals, and black fruits<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Li, C. L. et al. Cerasus in Flora of China. Science Press. 9, 404â420 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d50938e678">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Yi, X. G. The variation and phylogeography of Cerasus serrulata Mill. populations. J. Nanjing For. Univ. 14, 166â172 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d50938e681">5</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#Fig1">1</a>). <i>Cerasus serrulata</i> is the parent of many ornamental cherry varieties and is an important flowering cherry germplasm resource used for ornamental purposes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Wang, X. R. An illustrated monograph of cherry cultivars in China. Science Press. 12, 24â28 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d50938e691">6</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Images of a Chinese flowering cherry tree and its flowers and berries from March to April 2019."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Images of a Chinese flowering cherry tree and its flowers and berries from March to April 2019.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00382-1/MediaObjects/41438_2020_382_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00382-1/MediaObjects/41438_2020_382_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="515" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>This Chinese flowering cherry tree grows in Baohua mountain, Jiangsu Province. The lower right corner shows a close-up of cherry flowers and berries</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p><i>Cerasus serrulata</i> is widely distributed in the midwestern and eastern regions of China and possibly also is present on the Korean Peninsula and in Japan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Liu, Z. X. et al. Development of stamens and carpels in single and double flowers of Cerasus serrulata. J. Beijing For. Univ. 32, 486â491 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d50938e719">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Yi, X. G. The variation and phylogeography of Cerasus serrulata Mill. populations. J. Nanjing For. Univ. 14, 166â172 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d50938e722">5</a></sup>. China has the most abundant germplasm resources of <i>C. serrulata</i>, where various natural and artificial hybrids have been developed and selected<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Ma, H., Olsen, R., Pooler, M. &amp; Kramer, M. Evaluation of flowering cherry species, hybrids, and cultivars using simple sequence repeat markers. SocHort Science. 134, 435â444 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d50938e729">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Knight, R. Abstract bibliography of fruit breeding and genetics to 1965, Prunus. Commonwealth Agricultural Bureau. 3, 752â824 (1969)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d50938e732">8</a></sup>. More than 45 species (31 endemics) and nine varieties occur in Southwest China, along the middle and lower reaches of the Yangtze River, in Northeast China, and in Chinese Taipei (there are ~16 species and 11 varieties in Japan)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Li, C. L. et al. Cerasus in Flora of China. Science Press. 9, 404â420 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d50938e736">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Iwatsuki, K., Boufford, D. E. &amp; Ohba, H. Flora of Japan. Science Press 2, 435â148 (2001)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d50938e739">9</a></sup>. Due to a long history of cultivation along with the naturalization of wild flowering cherry species and interspecific hybridization in China, there has been much taxonomic debate over the name, origin, and delimitation among different populations in <i>C. serrulata</i> (especially different populations in alpine areas). Numerous investigations into <i>C. serrulata</i>, including the collection of specimens, development of morphological markers and molecular markers, and assessment of evolutionary and phylogeographic relationships, have been carried out to determine its origin<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Meng, L. I. et al. Numeric and structural characteristics of Cerasus serrulata population around the high-elevation wetlands of Dayangshan. J. Nanjing For. Univ. 37, 40â44 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d50938e750">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Hong, Z. et al. Application of the molecular marker technology to Cerasus Mill. (Rosaceae). World For. Res. 31, 16â24 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d50938e753">11</a></sup>. The interpretation of Li Chao-Luang and Bruce Bartholomew in the Flora of China was adopted here in dealing with taxonomic disputes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Li, C. L. et al. Cerasus in Flora of China. Science Press. 9, 404â420 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d50938e757">4</a></sup>. Considering that genomics-based research could be fundamentally helpful in overcoming this drawback to some extent, a de novo genome sequencing project was launched in this study, aiming to provide a scientific and theoretical basis for understanding <i>C. serrulata</i>.</p><p>The acquisition of a high-quality genome may provide more detailed insights into the evolutionary history and contribute toward the settlement of disputes over the classification. In the past decade, whole-genome sequencing has been widely performed on a number of Rosaceae species, including the fruit crop species sweet cherry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Shirasawa, K. et al. The genome sequence of sweet cherry (Prunus avium) for use in genomics-assisted breeding. DNA Research. 24, 499â508 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d50938e767">12</a></sup>, peach<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Verde, I. et al. The high-quality draft genome of peach (Prunus persica) identifies unique patterns of genetic diversity, domestication and genome evolution. Nat. Genet. 45, 487â494 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d50938e771">13</a></sup>, apple<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Velasco, R. et al. The genome of the domesticated apple (Malus x domestica). Nat. Genet. 42, 833â839 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d50938e775">14</a></sup>, Chinese plum<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Velasco, D. et al. Evolutionary genomics of peach and almond domestication. G3 32, 116â121 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d50938e779">15</a></sup>, strawberry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Shulaev, V. et al. The genome of woodland strawberry (Fragaria vesca). Nat. Genet. 43, 109â116 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d50938e783">16</a></sup>, apricot<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Jiang, F. et al. The apricot (Prunus armeniaca L.) genome elucidates Rosaceae evolution and beta-carotenoid synthesis. Hortic. Res. 6, 128â134 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d50938e788">17</a></sup>, and black raspberry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Vanburen, R. et al. The genome of black raspberry (Rubus occidentalis). Plant J. 87, 535â547 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d50938e792">18</a></sup>, as well as the ornamental species <i>Prunus</i> x <i>yedoensis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Seunghoon, B. et al. Draft genome sequence of wild Prunus yedoensis reveals massive inter-specific hybridization between sympatric flowering cherries. Genome Biol. 19, 45â51 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d50938e801">19</a></sup>, plum blossom<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Zhang, Q. et al. The genome of Prunus mume. Nat. Commun. 3, 1318â1325 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d50938e805">20</a></sup> Chines rose<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Lin, W. et al. Characterization of the complete chloroplast genome of Chinese rose, Rosa chinensis (Rosaceae: Rosa). Mitochondrial DNA B. 4, 51â58 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d50938e810">21</a></sup>, and multiflora rose<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Hibrand, S. L. et al. A high-quality genome sequence of Rosa chinensis to elucidate ornamental traits. Nat. Plants 4, 36â43 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d50938e814">22</a></sup>. In this study, we successfully assembled the genome of <i>C. serrulata</i> (2<i>n</i>â=â2<i>x</i>â=â16) and compared it with the genomes of closely related species in terms of the gene family, positive gene selection, and phylogeny. This work provides a foundation for clarifying the genetic variation, genetic diversity, and genealogical structure of <i>C. serrulata</i>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome estimation and assembly</h3><p>The genome size of <i>C. serrulata</i> was estimated by <i>k</i>-mer analysis to be 256.65âMb with repeat and heterozygosity percentages of 46.55% and 1.67%, respectively (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#MOESM1">S1</a>). In this work, 1.88 million clean reads (~47.99âGb) were obtained, with a read N50 of 32.08 bb and a read mean length of 25.49 bb (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#Tab1">1</a>). The results of BLAST with randomly selected reads aligned to the nucleotide sequence database (Nt) were used to assess contamination. A total of 43.60% and 25.59% of mapped reads on the alignment were represented by <i>P. mume</i> and <i>P. persica</i>, respectively (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#MOESM1">S2</a>). A Nanopore long-read distribution was computed using different gradient lengths (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#MOESM1">S1</a>). Primary assembly was employed by Canu v1.5, WTDBG v1.1, and SMARTdenovo, with adjusted parameters, resulting in an optimized primary assembled genome of 265.38âMb distributed across 182 contigs.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Statistics and characteristics of the genome of <i>C. serrulata</i></b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>In total, 122.84 million paired reads were generated from Hi-C, of which 95.72 million paired reads (77.92%) were mapped to the primary assembly, and 53.35 million paired reads (43.43%) were uniquely mapped. Furthermore, 41.20 million valid interaction pairs were strongly mapped to the unique mapped paired reads, accounting for 77.23% (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#MOESM1">S2</a>). A total of 182 primary contigs were fragmented and reassembled using the Lachesis combined Hi-C data. A total of 263.16âMb genomic sequences, accounting for 99.16% of total contig sequences, were located. Two hundred and eighty-nine corrected contigs were clustered into eight groups using the agglomerative hierarchical clustering method. Among them, 245 ordered contigs (252.25âMb) were anchored with a defined order and orientation (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#MOESM1">S3</a>).</p><p>The final unambiguous chromosomal-level genome of <i>C. serrulata</i> with no obvious assembly errors was composed of eight clusters, as indicated in the Hi-C interaction heatmap (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#Fig2">2</a>). The chromosomal genome of <i>C. serrulata</i> is 265.40âMb in length and is characterized by 304 contigs and 67 scaffolds, with a contig N50 of 1.56âMb and a scaffold N50 of 31.12âMb (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#Tab1">1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Hi-C interaction heatmap for Cerasus serrulata."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Hi-C interaction heatmap for <i>Cerasus serrulata.</i></b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00382-1/MediaObjects/41438_2020_382_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00382-1/MediaObjects/41438_2020_382_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="689" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Chr01â08 are the abbreviations of chromosome 1â8</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genome assessment and annotation</h3><p>BUSCO analysis showed that, of the 1614 expected embryophytic genes (embryophyta_odb10), 1528 (94.67%) complete BUSCO genes were identified, and 1352 genes were complete single-copy genes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#MOESM1">S4</a>). CEGMA analysis indicated that of the 458 conserved core genes in eukaryotes, 447 (97.60%) core genes were identified based on sequence similarity (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#MOESM1">S4</a>). Approximately 254.77 million reads (98.08%) for Illumina were mapped to the assembly, and the number of mapped paired reads was ~230.62 million reads (88.79%) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#MOESM1">S4</a>).</p><p>A high degree of consistency among the BUSCO and CEGMA data and the mapping ratio for Illumina confirms the high quality of the assembled genome. The repetitive sequences were 130.11âMb in total length, representing 49.02% of the genome (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#Tab1">1</a>). Approximately 63.20âMb of long terminal repeats (LTRs), representing 48.57% of all repeat types, were dominant. <i>Copia</i> (25.27âMb) and <i>Gypsy</i> (34.23âMb) were the most abundant subtypes, representing 19.42% and 26.31%, respectively. The characteristics of the repeats, including the number, length, and percentage of each repeat type, were captured in detail (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#MOESM1">S5</a>).</p><p>Ab initio-, homology-based-, and transcriptome-based predictions were achieved using various workflows (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#MOESM1">S6</a>). The results of the predictions were integrated into the final nonredundant protein-coding genes using EVM (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#Tab1">1</a>). These 29,094 (104.75âMb) coding genes were composed of 157,063 exons, with an average gene length of 3.61âkb. For genome annotation, 27,611 genes, accounting for 94.90% of predicted coding genes, were annotated in at least one functional database. Gene annotation numbers and percentages were calculated by aligning to all the databases (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#MOESM1">S7</a>), and each gene annotation is provided in great detail in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#MOESM2">S8</a>. In addition, the <i>C. serrulata</i> genome was characterized by 2170 pseudogenes, 94 microRNAs, 315 rRNAs, and 472 tRNAs (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#MOESM3">S9</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Synteny analysis between <i>C. serrulata</i> and both <i>P. persica</i> and <i>C. avium</i>
</h3><p>Two highly consistent colinear maps were constructed by comparing the <i>C. serrulata</i> genome with the <i>P. persica</i> and <i>C. avium</i> genomes. The colinear map indicated that the sequences of the <i>C. serrulata</i> genome were practically identical to those of the <i>P. persica</i> genome, as indicated by the red lines on the diagonal (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#Fig3">3a</a>). Only a few spots of blue color represent identical sequences of opposite orientation between the two genomes. Interestingly, the colinear map of <i>C. serrulata</i> and <i>C. avium</i> showed that they possessed numerous identical sequences on different chromosomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#Fig3">3b</a>). Among their differences, identical sequences in opposite orientations constituted a relatively high proportion. The map indicated that, compared with the <i>P. persica</i> genome, the <i>C. serrulata</i> genome is of extraordinarily high quality. The spots on the colinear map may exist and might have caused some functional changes between the two species or otherwise could have resulted from assembly errors. The syntenic blocks generated by comparing the <i>C. avium</i> genome with the <i>C. serrulata</i> genome were distributed across eight similar chromosomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#Fig3">3c</a>). We obtained 333 syntenic blocks, which were composed of 14,072 genes, across the two genomes (Supplementary Tables S10 and S11). Among the 333 syntenic blocks, there are 193 common blocks with an accurate match on the same chromosome, representing 57.96% of the total blocks. Thirty-six blocks on chromosome 04 of <i>C. serrulata</i> were discovered in <i>C. avium</i>, of which 33 occurred on chromosome 04 of <i>C. avium</i>. The results also indicated that 58 blocks on chromosome 01 of <i>C. serrulata</i> were distributed on all of the chromosomes of <i>C. avium</i> and were not found on other chromosomes in <i>C. serrulata</i>. These findings imply that chromosome 04 of <i>C. serrulata</i> is relatively conserved, while chromosome 01 is the most ancient or has been the most active chromosome throughout evolution.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Synteny analysis via comparisons of Cerasus serrulata with Prunus persica and Cerasus avium."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Synteny analysis via comparisons of <i>Cerasus serrulata</i> with <i>Prunus persica</i> and <i>Cerasus avium</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00382-1/MediaObjects/41438_2020_382_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00382-1/MediaObjects/41438_2020_382_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="370" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b> Colinear map of the <i>C. serrulata</i> genome and <i>P. persica</i> genome. The red color represents identical sequences in the same orientation, while the blue color represents identical sequences in the opposite orientation. <b>b</b> Colinear map of the <i>C. serrulata</i> genome and <i>C. avium</i> genome. The red color represents identical sequences in the same orientation, while the blue color represents identical sequences in the opposite orientation. <b>c</b> Syntenic blocks of <i>C. serrulata</i> and <i>C. avium</i></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Evolutionary analysis of <i>C. serrulata</i>
</h3><p>Based on the combined results from BLASTP and Pfam searches, gene families were identified and subjected to comparative genomic analysis to reveal their evolutionary relationships. The results revealed that there were 14,792 gene families in the <i>C. serrulata</i> genome and that clustered with the annotated 29,094 protein-coding genes. Among the 14,792 gene families, 4451 gene families were unique or specific to the <i>C. serrulata</i> genomes compared with the genomes of <i>C. avium</i> and <i>P. yedoensis</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#MOESM1">S3a</a>). The 4451 gene families clustered with 5671 genes annotated by Pfam and other databases (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#MOESM4">S12</a>). The enrichment results for GO functional analysis involved mainly transmembrane transport, endonuclease activities, and triphosphate biosynthetic processes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#MOESM1">S13</a>). KEGG functional analysis revealed enrichment in terms related to oxidative phosphorylation and meiosis (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#MOESM1">S13</a>). These results suggest that <i>C. serrulata</i> has a tendency for cell division and reproduction and undergoes phosphorylation processes. Furthermore, we obtained all types of features of the gene families, including single-copy orthologs, multiple-copy orthologs, other orthologs, and unique genes, present in the related species included in the phylogenetic tree construction (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#MOESM1">S3b</a>).</p><p>Seven hundred forty expanded gene families and 1031 contracted gene families were identified by analyzing the differences between the ancestral species and <i>C. serrulata</i> using CAFE v4.0 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#Fig4">4a</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#MOESM5">S14</a>). The expanded and contracted family genes were significantly enriched (<i>q</i>â&lt;â0.05) in 232 and 266 GO terms of the three categories, respectively (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#MOESM6">S15</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#MOESM7">S16</a>). A total of 228 rapidly evolving gene families consisting of 2023 genes were significantly enriched (<i>q</i>â&lt;â0.01) in 269 GO terms (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#MOESM8">S17</a>). The top functional terms involve oxidationâreduction, glycerolipid biosynthetic processes, glycerolipid and lipid metabolism, and so forth. The KEGG enrichment analysis indicated that rapidly evolving genes were relevant to the metabolism of glycine, serine, threonine, phenylalanine, beta-alanine, and tyrosine (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#MOESM1">S4</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Analysis of the evolution of the Cerasus serrulata genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Analysis of the evolution of the <i>Cerasus serrulata</i> genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00382-1/MediaObjects/41438_2020_382_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00382-1/MediaObjects/41438_2020_382_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="637" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b> Phylogenetic tree; divergence time; and profiles of gene families that underwent expansion, contraction, and rapid evolution in ten species. <b>b</b> Ka/Ks distribution of paralogs and orthologs of <i>C. serrulata</i> compared with that of <i>C. avium</i> and <i>P. yedoensis</i>. <b>c</b> Distribution of 4DTv distances in <i>C. serrulata</i>, <i>C. avium</i>, and <i>P. yedoensis</i></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Gene family analysis revealed that 3754 genes were similarly present in all the species, and 656 of these genes were single-copy genes. The phylogenetic tree composed of ten species was generated using 656 single-copy orthologs. The tree indicated that <i>C. serrulata</i> was most closely related to <i>P. yedoensis</i>, which speciated ~17.34 million years ago (Mya), while the divergence time estimated between <i>C. serrulata</i> and <i>C. avium</i> was ~21.44âMya (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#Fig4">4a</a>). These results demonstrated the close evolutionary relationship between <i>C. serrulata</i> and <i>P. yedoensis</i>. The phylogenetic tree convincingly showed that the time scale of <i>Cerasus</i> and <i>Prunus</i> was close, diverging ~28.14âMya. This verified the taxonomic claim that <i>Cerasus</i> is independent of <i>Prunus</i>. In addition, the relationship of <i>Rosa</i> L., represented by <i>R. chinensis</i> and <i>R. multiflora</i>, to <i>Rubus</i> L., represented by <i>R. occidentalis</i>, suggested speciation from ~90.80âMya. All of the genera shared common ancestors and diverged at ~175.51âMya. These results are consistent with the observed morphological similarities and differences in the genera.</p><p>The homologous gene pairs obtained by MUSCLE were subjected to the KaKs Calculator to calculate each gene pair value. As reflected by the Ka/Ks-density curves of <i>C. serrulata</i> vs. <i>P. yedoensis</i> and <i>C. serrulata</i> vs. <i>C. avium</i>, the differences in numbers for the two groups were not large (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#Fig4">4b</a>). The positively selected genes (Ka/Ks &gt;1) for <i>C. serrulata</i> vs. <i>C. avium</i> and for <i>C. serrulata</i> vs. <i>P. yedoensis</i> were composed of 136 and 133 gene pairs, respectively (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#MOESM9">S18</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#MOESM10">S19</a>). Transcription factors with definitively matched Pfam domains were identified from these gene pairs and screened to detect their functions. Ten types of transcription factors were found for <i>C. serrulata</i> compared to <i>C. avium</i>, and eight types were found for <i>C. serrulata</i> compared to <i>P. yedoensis</i> (Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#Tab2">2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#Tab3">3</a>). The common transcription factors, including ERF, B3, TCP, bHLH, and NAC types, suggest that these genes are more likely to be positively selected in the process of growth and development, physiological metabolism, and stress responses in <i>C. serrulata</i>. The 4DTv values were estimated by comparison with themselves or with each other and indicated that they had experienced only one WGD event (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#Fig4">4c</a>). Self-comparison peaks at 0.014 (<i>C. serrulata</i>), 0.011 (<i>P. yedoensis</i>), and 0.028 (<i>C. avium</i>) indicated a common WGD event. The peaks of the mutual comparisons appeared slightly earlier than those of the self-comparisons, implying that they had begun to differentiate after the end of the whole-genome replication event. The divergence times of the three species were close: the peak of <i>C. serrulata</i> vs. <i>C. avium</i> was calculated to be 0.0088, and the peak of <i>C. serrulata</i> vs. <i>P. yedoensis</i> was calculated to be 0.0069. This suggests that the divergence of <i>C. avium</i> occurred earlier than did that of <i>P. yedoensis</i>, which is in accordance with the phylogenetic tree results.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 Positively selected genes by <i>C. serrulata</i> compared to <i>C. avium</i></b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-3"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab3" data-test="table-caption">Table 3 Positively selected genes by <i>C. serrulata</i> compared to <i>P. yedoensis</i></b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1/tables/3"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">MADS-box gene family in <i>C. serrulata</i>
</h3><p>MADS-box transcription factors control multiple traits in plants and are best known for their regulation of plant floral organ development by type II MIKCc MADS-box genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Fei, C. et al. Evolutionary analysis of MIKCc-type MADS-box genes in gymnosperms and angiosperms. Front. Plant Sci. 8, 895â899 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d50938e2445">23</a></sup>. The MADS-box gene family has been thoroughly described in Rosaceae genomes, such as those of <i>P. mume</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Zhang, Q. et al. The genome of Prunus mume. Nat. Commun. 3, 1318â1325 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d50938e2451">20</a></sup>, <i>P. persica</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Wells, C. E. et al. A genome-wide analysis of MADS-box genes in peach. BMC Plant Biol. 15, 41â46 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d50938e2457">24</a></sup>, and <i>M. domestica</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Tian, Y. et al. Genome-wide identification and analysis of the MADS-box gene family in apple. Gene 555, 277â290 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d50938e2463">25</a></sup>. The MADS-box family was identified by aligning to <i>A. thaliana</i> using MAFFT 7.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Katoh, K., Rozewicki, J. &amp; Yamada, K. D. MAFFT online service: multiple sequence alignment, interactive sequence choice and visualization. Brief. Bioinform. 4, 20â26 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d50938e2471">26</a></sup>, and the tree was constructed by FastTree v2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Morgan, N. P., Paramvir, S. D. &amp; Adam, P. A. FastTree2-approximately maximum-likelihood trees for large alignments. PLOS ONE 3, 5â12 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d50938e2475">27</a></sup>, with the default parameters. Here, we identified a total of 148 members of MADS-box genes in the genome of <i>C. serrulata</i>, including 44 type I MADS-box genes and 104 type II MADS-box genes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#MOESM11">S20</a>). Compared with the number of previously reported MADS-box genes in other Rosaceae species, such as <i>P. persica</i> (79) and <i>M. domestica</i> (146), the number of MADS-box genes in <i>C. serrulata</i> was evidently greater than that in <i>P. persica</i>. This suggests that the <i>C. serrulata</i> floral organs are relatively well developed, while <i>P. persica</i> floral organ development may have been limited by the deletion of some MADS-box genes. The type II MADS-box genes were classified into SVP, AGL15, ANR1, AGL12, AG, STK, AP1, AGL6, SEP1/3, SEP2/4, FLC, TM3, TM8, AP3, and PI subfamilies, in accordance with the classification of <i>A. thaliana</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#Fig5">5</a>). However, we did not detect any members of the AGL32 subfamily in <i>C. serrulata</i>, suggesting the loss of several functions that may affect seed development occurred, such as the maternal role in fertilization. In addition, the most expanded subfamily was the SVP subfamily, which expanded to 32 members in <i>C. serrulata</i> (there are only four members in <i>A. thaliana</i>). Considering that this family plays important roles in early flowering in early spring, we postulate that the expansion of the SVP subfamily is correlated with flowering time control in <i>C. serrulata</i>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="The MADS-box gene family in Cerasus serrulata and Arabidopsis thaliana."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: The MADS-box gene family in <i>Cerasus serrulata</i> and <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00382-1/MediaObjects/41438_2020_382_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00382-1/MediaObjects/41438_2020_382_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="653" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>The red color represents genes from <i>Cerasus serrulata</i>, and the green color represents genes from <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8"><b>MYB</b><b>and</b><b>WRKY</b><b>gene</b> families in <i>C. serrulata</i>, <i>C. avium</i>, and <i>P. yedoensis</i>
</h3><p>The members of other gene families, such as those of MYB and WRKY transcription factors, play important roles in many biological functions and are widely distributed in plant roots, stems, leaves, and flowers. Members of the MYB and WRKY gene families containing distinct protein domains were identified by alignment to the Pfam and Swiss-Prot database using Pfam and phmmer searches. Phylogenetic trees for the MYB and WRKY gene families were constructed using <i>C. serrulata</i>, <i>C. avium</i>, and <i>P. yedoensis</i> data. The members of the MYB gene family grouped into seven subfamilies (subfamily aâg) containing 1132 genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#Fig6">6</a>). Among 1132 genes, 372 originated from <i>C. serrulata</i>, 429 originated from <i>C. avium</i>, and 331 originated from <i>P. yedoensis</i>. Specifically, subfamily a has 71 members with the same or similar protein domain, and subfamilies bâg have 89, 60, 145, 171, 96, and 500 members, respectively (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#MOESM12">S21</a>). For the WRKY gene family, the same method was adopted to ensure the domain and classify the subfamilies. Eight subfamilies (subfamilies aâh) composed of 221 genes were generated and clustered with regularity (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#MOESM1">S5</a>). Sixty-six genes in <i>C. serrulata</i> were present in eight subfamilies, while 77 and 78 genes were found in <i>C. avium</i> and <i>P. yedoensis</i>, respectively (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#MOESM13">S22</a>). The acquisition of a gene family implies species-specific tandem duplication and gene loss events during evolution that may have led to species-specific functional deterioration.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="The MYB gene family could be divided into seven subfamilies, namely, subfamilies aâg."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6: The MYB gene family could be divided into seven subfamilies, namely, subfamilies aâg.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00382-1/MediaObjects/41438_2020_382_Fig6_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00382-1/MediaObjects/41438_2020_382_Fig6_HTML.png" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="682" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>The red members represent genes from <i>C. serrulata</i>, the blue members represent genes from <i>C. avium</i>, and the green members represent genes from <i>P. yedoensis</i></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9"><b>Plant disease-resistance genes (</b>R-genes<b>) in the Rosaceae</b>
</h3><p>To identify and classify plant disease-resistance genes (R-genes) in Rosaceae species, nine species involved in the gene family analysis were analyzed by aligning to the PRGdb (<a href="http://prgdb.crg.eu/wiki/Main_Page">http://prgdb.crg.eu/wiki/Main_Page</a>) using the BLAST program. In total, 941 R-genes were identified in <i>C. serrulata</i>, which was more than that identified in <i>P. yedoensis</i> (555) but less than that identified in <i>C. avium</i> (1149) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#MOESM14">S23</a>). The common and functionally confirmed R-gene types mainly included five major types: CNLs, TNLs, RLKs, RLPs, and Mlos (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#Fig7">7</a>). Among all of the types, 161 and 180 TNL R-genes are present in <i>C. serrulata</i> and <i>C. avium</i>, while only four genes were found in <i>P. yedoensis</i>. Of all the representative species, <i>P. persica</i> has 1123 R-genes, and <i>R. occidentalis</i> has 187 R-genes, which are two species with the most and the least R-genes, respectively. For many plants, resistance is considered one of the most important traits that is controlled by R-genes and is affected by the environment. Our results provide fundamental information on the R-genes in these species, which are a prerequisite for resistance.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-7" data-title="Plant disease-resistance genes (R-genes) identified in nine representative species of Rosaceae."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig7" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 7: Plant disease-resistance genes (R-genes) identified in nine representative species of <i>Rosaceae.</i></b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1/figures/7" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00382-1/MediaObjects/41438_2020_382_Fig7_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-7-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00382-1/MediaObjects/41438_2020_382_Fig7_HTML.png" alt="figure7" loading="lazy" width="685" height="370" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-7-desc"><p>The major types, CNLs, TNLs, RLKs, RLPs, and Mlos were identified in nine species</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1/figures/7" data-track-dest="link:Figure7 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p>The complete genomes of <i>C. avium</i> and <i>P. yedoensis</i> were previously sequenced and published<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Shirasawa, K. et al. The genome sequence of sweet cherry (Prunus avium) for use in genomics-assisted breeding. DNA Research. 24, 499â508 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d50938e2740">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Seunghoon, B. et al. Draft genome sequence of wild Prunus yedoensis reveals massive inter-specific hybridization between sympatric flowering cherries. Genome Biol. 19, 45â51 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d50938e2743">19</a></sup>. As an important germplasm resource of <i>Cerasus</i>, <i>C. serrulata</i> is cultivated worldwide for its beautiful blossoms and superior ornamental characteristics. Resolving the genome sequence of <i>C. serrulata</i> is thus of great significance. Genome assembly can be relatively challenging due to high heterozygosity percentages and high proportions of repeat sequences. However, with the development of long-read sequencing techniques, the assemblies of heterozygous genomes have been largely successful<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Bayliss, S. C. et al. The use of Oxford Nanopore native barcoding for complete genome\r, assembly. GigaScience 6, 1â6 (2017)." href="#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d50938e2757">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Goodwin, S. et al. Oxford Nanopore sequencing, hybrid error correction, and de novo assembly of a eukaryotic genome. Genome Res. 25, 1750â1758 (2015)." href="#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d50938e2757_1">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Vaser, R. et al. Fast and accurate de novo genome assembly from long uncorrected reads. Genome Res. 5, 27â32 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d50938e2760">30</a></sup>. In this work, we de novo assembled the <i>C. serrulata</i> genome with a chromosomal-level assembly using a combination of Nanopore and Hi-C sequencing techniques. The assembled genome is 265.4âMb in length, which is smaller than both the <i>C. avium</i> genome (272.4âMb) and <i>P. yedoensis</i> genome (323.8âMb). The contig and scaffold N50 values of 1.56 and 31.12âMb, respectively, are higher than those of <i>C. avium</i> (scaffold N50â=â291.57âkb) and <i>P. yedoensis</i> (contig N50â=â132.59âkb, scaffold N50â=â198.95âkb). These results demonstrate that a high-quality <i>C. serrulata</i> genome was generated and could serve as a credible whole-genome reference sequence for research into the flowering cherry.</p><p>The <i>magnum opus</i> of Linnaeus, Species Plantarum, included the initial classification of <i>Prunus</i>, which included cherry, plum, peach, plum, apricot, and so on<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Brummitt, R. K. et al. The species plantarum project, an international collaborative initiative for higher plant taxonomy. Taxon 50, 1217â1230 (2001)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d50938e2792">31</a></sup>. The earliest taxonomic history of <i>Cerasus</i> can be traced back to 1753, and <i>Cerasus</i> was suggested to be independent of <i>Prunus</i> in 1754<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Miller, P. containing the methods of cultivating and improving the kitchen, fruit and flower garden, as also the physick garden, wilderness, conservatory, and vineyard. Gardeners Dictionary 3, 576â614 (1753)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d50938e2806">32</a></sup>. Since then, the parallel existence of <i>Prunus</i> and <i>Cerasus</i> has been accepted. However, the application of molecular techniques has indicated that the <i>Cerasus</i> subgenus should belong to a single genus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Li, C. L. et al. Cerasus in Flora of China. Science Press. 9, 404â420 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d50938e2819">4</a></sup>. The <i>C. serrulata</i> genome and the comparative analysis of related species suggest that <i>P. yedoensis</i> should be classified as <i>C. yedoensis</i>. Moreover, the phylogenetic tree demonstrated that <i>C. serrulata</i>, <i>C. avium</i>, and <i>P. yedoensis</i> clustered together with the shortest divergence time and were distinct from other species of <i>Prunus</i>. The relatively recent differentiation of <i>C. serrulata</i> and <i>P. yedoensis</i> suggests that they are evolutionarily closely related. 4DTv analysis revealed that <i>C. serrulata</i>, <i>C. avium</i>, and <i>P. yedoensis</i> experienced one common WGD event and that the divergence of <i>C. avium</i> occurred earlier than that of <i>P. yedoensis</i>. Thus, <i>C. serrulata</i> and <i>P. yedoensis</i> diverged relatively recently, while <i>C. avium</i> diverged from the ancestor of these two species much earlier.</p><p>The colinear map revealed a high chromosome-level assembly of the <i>C. serrulata</i> genome in comparison with the <i>P. persica</i> genome. The colinear map of the <i>C. serrulata</i> genome and <i>C. avium</i> genome showed that they had many identical sequences on different chromosomes, evidently more than that in the colinear map of <i>C. serrulata</i> and <i>P. persica</i>. A previous analysis based on a phylogenetic tree and 4DTv analysis convincingly demonstrated that the evolutionary relationship of <i>C. serrulata</i> and <i>C. avium</i> was closer than that of <i>C. serrulata</i> and <i>P. persica</i>. We postulate that, even though there are differences between the <i>C. serrulata</i> and <i>C. avium</i> genomes, they do not exceed the differences between the <i>C. serrulata</i> and <i>P. persica</i> genomes. The only reasonable explanation is that there could be assembly errors in the genomes of <i>C. serrulata</i> and <i>C. avium</i>. The assembly quality of <i>C. serrulata</i> was verified in several ways, including via BUSCO, the mapping ratio for Illumina, and the colinear map of <i>C. serrulata</i> and <i>P. persica</i>. However, further evidence or experiments are still needed to confirm this.</p><p><i>Cerasus serrulata</i> is cultivated for its flowers, while <i>C. avium</i> is cultivated for its fruit; thus, an assessment of the characteristics of its genomes is warranted. Based on synteny analysis, we discovered 333 syntenic blocks sharing 14,072 syntenic genes. Among these genes, eight chromosomes containing 12,397 genes demonstrated a one-to-one correspondence. No large-scale chromosome translocation or chromosome rearrangement was observed between the two genomes, implying that <i>C. serrulata</i> is relatively evolutionarily conserved. In addition, we studied the commonness and intricacies of <i>C. serrulata</i>, <i>C. avium</i>, and <i>P. yedoensis</i>, including their gene families, positively selected genes, and plant disease-resistance genes (R-genes), and focused on the identification and clustering of the MYB and WRKY family genes in particular. We enriched specific family genes and positively selected genes to determine the key aspects of differentiating <i>C. serrulata</i>. The results showed that <i>P. yedoensis</i> had only 554 R-genes, while <i>C. avium</i> and <i>P. yedoensis</i> had 1149 and 941 R-genes, respectively. Among the five major types, the number of type TNL R-genes differed significantly, and <i>C. serrulata</i>, <i>C. avium</i>, and <i>P. yedoensis</i> were found to have 161, 180, and four TNL R-genes, respectively. <i>Prunus yedoensis</i> is a hybrid of <i>P. speciosa</i> and <i>P. subhirtella</i>, and is an important cultivar for ornamental purposes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Innan, H., Terauchi, R., Miyashita, N. T. &amp; Tsunewaki, K. DNA fingerprinting study on the intraspecific variation and the origin of Prunus yedoensis (Someiyoshino). Jpn. J. Genet. 70, 185â196 (1995)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d50938e2992">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Kato, S. et al. Origins of Japanese flowering cherry (Prunus subgenus Cerasus) cultivars revealed using nuclear SSR markers. Tree Genet. Genomes 10, 477â487 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d50938e2995">34</a></sup>. This remarkable contrast illustrates that many R-genes for <i>P. yedoensis</i> have been lost, such that its ability to survive in a harsh environment may decrease during the process of artificial selection and hybridization. R-genes play essential roles in breeding processes, and their acquisition enables us to determine on which resistant traits different types of R-genes govern.</p><p>Chinese flowering cherry is an important ornamental tree species during early spring and is widely planted in gardens. Understanding the regulation of floral organ development will help improve the varieties and explain the diversity in flowering cherry. The MADS-box gene family is known to be related to floral organ development. We identified 148 MADS-box members in the genome of <i>C. serrulata</i>; these members included 44 type I MADS-box genes and 104 type II MADS-box genes. Within the type II MADS-box genes, the loss of the AGL32 subfamily might affect seed development. The SVP subfamily expanded to 32 members in <i>C. serrulata</i>, whereas only four members disappeared in <i>A. thaliana</i>. The functions of the SVP subfamily members are related to early flowering, and the expansion event implies that these members may influence or control flowering time. Our results presented here may provide new insights into the current problems associated with genetic diversity and offer valuable information for evolutionary studies on <i>C. serrulata</i> and other <i>Cerasus</i> species.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Materials and methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Materials and methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Plant materials and extraction of DNA</h3><p><i>C. serrulata</i> trees were grown in Baohua Provincial Nature Reserve, Jurong, Jiangsu (119.05 E, 32.06âN, 240âm above sea level). Fresh leaves and other tissues were collected for the deposition of specimens at the Herbarium of Nanjing Forestry University (specimen number: YXG18032501) and for sequencing at Biomarker Technologies Corporation, Beijing. High-quality genomic DNA was extracted from the leaves using a DNeasy Plant Mini Kit (Qiagen, Germany). The concentration and purity of the extracted DNA were assessed using a Nanodrop 2000 spectrophotometer (Thermo, MA, USA) and Qubit 3.0 (Thermo, CA, USA), and the integrity of the DNA was measured using pulsed-field electrophoresis with 0.8% agarose gel.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec13">Library preparation and sequencing</h3><p>A short-read library was prepared with a Nextera XT Library Prep Kit (Illumina, CA, USA). Approximately 1âÎ¼g of genomic DNA was extracted for short-read library construction and sheared to short fragment sizes of ~350âbp using a Covaris S2 sonicator (Covaris, MA, USA). The qualified library was sequenced on an Illumina X-ten platform (Illumina, CA, USA) with a paired-end 150 (PE150) read layout. In total, 38.14âGb of clean data (~148.62âÃâthe assembled genome) were generated and used for genome survey and correction.</p><p>Approximately 10âÎ¼g of genomic DNA was prepared for long-read library construction using an ONT Template Prep Kit (Nanopore, Oxford, UK). According to the manufacturerâs instructions, the total DNA was sheared to sizes of ~20âkb, and the sheared fragments were recovered by a BluePippinâ¢ System using the processes of damage repair, end repair, and blunt-end adapter ligation via a NEBNext FFPE DNA Repair Mix Kit. The qualified library was subsequently sequenced on the Nanopore PromethION platform with an R9 cell sequencing reagent kit.</p><p>For Hi-C library construction, samples were digested with a restriction enzyme (HindIII), in situ labeled with a biotinylated residue, and end repaired. Purified DNA was sheared to a length of 300â700âbp using a NEBNext Ultra II DNA Library Prep Kit (Illumina). The remaining data consisted of 122.84 million clean reads (~36.77âGb) following the removal of low-quality reads.</p><p>Four tissues (leaves, buds, flowers, and roots) collected from the same <i>C. serrulata</i> tree were mixed together for transcriptome sequencing. All of the Illumina and Nanopore sequencing data were deposited in the SRA database under NCBI BioProject ID PRJNA596558. The assembly and annotation data that supported the findings of this study have been deposited in the Figshare database, accessible via the following respective URLs: <a href="https://doi.org/10.6084/m9.figshare.12431846.v3">https://doi.org/10.6084/m9.figshare.12431846.v3</a> and <a href="https://doi.org/10.6084/m9.figshare.12431864.v3">https://doi.org/10.6084/m9.figshare.12431864.v3</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec14">Estimation of genomic features</h3><p>Short reads for Illumina were filtered by Fastp v0.19.3<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Chen, S. et al. fastp: an ultra-fast all-in-one FASTQ preprocessor. Bioinformatics 34, 884â890 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d50938e3077">35</a></sup>, after which they were randomly selected and prepared for contamination assessment using BLAST v2.2.31<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Altschul, S. F. et al. Basic local alignment search tool. J. Mol. Biol. 215, 403â410 (1990)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d50938e3081">36</a></sup> with an <i>E</i>-valueâ=â1e<sup>â05</sup>. We estimated overall genomic features, namely, size, heterozygosity, and repeats, by plotting the 19-mer depth distribution (<i>k</i>â=â19) using Jellyfish v2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Marcais, G. &amp; Kingsford, C. A fast, lock-free approach for efficient parallel counting of occurrences of k-mers. Bioinformatics. 27, 764â770 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d50938e3094">37</a></sup>. In brief, the average <i>k</i>-mer depth was divided by the total <i>k</i>-mer numbers to calculate the genomic features according to the formula. Repetitive sequences were estimated where the depth of the <i>k</i>-mer was more than two times that of the main peak, and heterozygous sequences were estimated where the depth was half of the main peak.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec15">Genome assembly</h3><p>The original offline data in FAST5 format obtained from the Nanopore PromethION platform were converted into fastq format using the Guppy procedure embedded in MinKNOW (Oxford Nanopore). Long reads that were low in quality and with short fragments (minimum length cutoff of 2000 bp) were filtered and removed using Fastp v0.19.3<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Chen, S. et al. fastp: an ultra-fast all-in-one FASTQ preprocessor. Bioinformatics 34, 884â890 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d50938e3115">35</a></sup>. Primary assemblies of the Nanopore long reads were mainly performed by Canu v1.5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Koren, S. et al. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 27, 722â736 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d50938e3119">38</a></sup> and WTDBG v1.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Lin, Y. et al. Assembly of long error-prone reads using de Bruijn graphs. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, 52â56 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d50938e3123">39</a></sup>, with adjusted parameters. Long reads were assembled into genomic contigs automatically using Canu v1.5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Koren, S. et al. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 27, 722â736 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00382-1#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d50938e3127">38</a></sup>, with the following parameters: genomeSizeâ=â300âm and corOutCoverageâ=â100.
